RATING: 7
Tile-laying game with great components, and multiple paths to victory.  Definitely has a similar feel to Attika (same designer) while still being a completely different game.  Works well as a 2-player, decent as a 3-player, haven't tried 4 players yet.

Each turn you lay a tile, and then HAVE to build (if you can't, you automatically lose).  When you're laying tiles, you might want to spread them out, and hope to quickly build the simpler buildings (huts), or you can layer them upwards, in an effort to build the more difficult towers.  There's another type of building to build as well, that is relatively easy to build either vertically or horizontally.  If you manage to build all of 2 building types, you win.  If the game runs until the end, then you want to have built the most of the most important building, then the most of the second-most important building, etc.

There is some interesting stategy in that you can destroy other player's settlements, but in many cases, it will be more beneficial to destroy your own.  Looks like a simple game, but there's definitely some things going on, as a plus, it can be a very quick and easy to play game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I think that the high chaos abstract multiplayer games just rub me the wrong way. I felt like my actions didn't really have a clear effect on the game, which, when combined with the high chaos, just frustrated me. This felt very similar to Clans in that sense.

So far, every Merkle game I've played I haven't liked (Attika, Funny Friends, Attribut). Still want to try Meuterer and Verrater, though, which sound pretty fun.

UPDATE: Upgraded to a 7 after playing the game 2-players. It plays much much better that way, like your usual two-player abstract strategy games (only with luck in the tile draws). It's also better with three versus four, though it'd harder to win since people can work together to prevent a win.

RATING: 8
This has the potential to replace Carcasonne as my tile-laying game of choice.  Game board development is tight even in a two-player game so player choices are constantly interacting.  I am thrilled with how the island develops, and good play is rewarded with more interesting island configurations.  The one drawback is that explaining the rules always makes the game sound more complicated than it really is, and the objective of the game is opaque to some at first (enough to turn off a couple people) because of the seemingly random multiple win conditions.  I would not be surprised if the game designers came up with a ruleset to create an island, and the win conditions were selected during play-testing to guide interesting island development rather than thematic consistency.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A fairly deep game.  Abstract in nature.  Place tiles and pieces according to the rules; try to get rid of 2 of the 3 types of pieces before anyone else.

Experienced players have a decided edge.  There's a good bit to be said for interfering with other people's plans, as well as for having back ups for your own.

I really like how this game develops.  It starts small and then balloons out to provide an increasing number of choices.  I also like how there's player interaction, but it's primarily indirect - as in interference rather than confrontation.  Also a relatively short game length.  Don't play with the AP-inclined.

A great, fun game that I strongly recommend.  Doesn't rate higher because, while strategic, the game is short.  Develops well over the course of the game, but the available options/choices are constrained and do not enable a player to pursue very [i]complex[/i] goals.  Excellent as a shorter abstract.

RATING: 7
In the distant past clans compete to build villages on a volcanic island which is constantly changing landscapes. Each turn, players place a new triangular tile to increase the size of Taluva or change the existing topography. Player's can add temples and towers and huts to the board in an attempt to score the most points. A great tactical and strategic game, easy to learn, the board is different every time which leads to no game being the same.

RATING: 7
In Carcassonne, what's on the tiles is all important and your meeples are just markers for claiming a score.  In Taluva terrain is really just a means to an end, which is creating settlements with your building pieces.  Tiles can be overlaid; indeed you will want to do that, both for the satisfaction of reducing  opponents' settlements, but also because towers can only be placed on (three-) high ground. Towers seem like the highest value buildings because they literally tower over everything and each player has just two.  In fact, it is the number of temples placed that can win the game.  I think that the geek voters are right and Taluva plays best with two.  Higher player counts bring a degree of chaos.  Taluva is a quick, thinky little neo-abstract, which is fine with me.

I own the Ferti edition, which improves the box artwork by replacing the constipated guy in a loin cloth of the HiG/Rio Grande edition.  The tiles and pieces are as beautiful as in the original.  Production quality is very good, with the tiles  nice and chunky.  The rules are clear and well set out.

RATING: 7.01
All games played 2-player only.  Rating is based on such.
Initially I dismissed this game and after the first two games. I was not impressed but I revisited it to give it a second chance.  It seemed as if there was too little to be done to stop an opponent, yet too much "take that".  My initial rating: 4After several more games I've become aware of the subtle strategies of both tile placement and settlement expansion.  The game has really proved to be very confrontational and efforts to control your opponents options must begin with tile and volcano arrangements.  Each game has increased my appreciation for this game.  I especially like the bits as well.  With a nice drawstring bag, this could be a nice portable game that is eye candy to the passerby as well.  Current rating now 6.5 and may go up further.For informational purposes; The game was initially played over lunch on sucessive days.  Two plays per.  With so little time between playings, it was easier to build on past experience to play better with each game.
Update: Played with variant using only 12 tiles per player and found the game more enjoyable.  Breaks the threshold between an OK game and a good game. Rating now 7+ after 6 games.

RATING: 8.5
This game has a nice blend of difficulty, play length and style.  It is both an area influence and tile laying game.  Strategies are subtle and players need to keep an eye on their options and what the opponents are doing.  It plays well with 2, 3, or 4, but strategy is slightly different with each.  In this regard it plays similar to Attika (also designed by the same designer).  Likewise, there are 2 methods to win - a quick win and a long win.  Inexperienced players will see quick wins, but long wins will be the norm for experienced players.  If you like tiles and area control - this game deserves a serious look.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
More than meets the eye. This is not "Carcassonne that stacks". It's a neat abstract that is dynamic whether you play with 2 or with 4.

RATING: 7
Taluva is a three-dimensional more strategic version of Carcassonne.
Compared to Carcassonne, it has a more strategic approach to tile laying: you need to "build" your chance to place the most difficult pieces during the course of several turns, during which you must disrupt other players' plays as well.

RATING: 8
[i]Posted: 12/2/07, Rated: 8[/i]
{2P: 8, 3P: 8, 4P: ???}

[i]Taluva[/i] is a new game that manages to be nostalgic.  I know that description sounds like a paradox, but when I play [i]Taluva[/i] I am reminded of other games.  The presence of volcanos and spending resources to climb higher are reminiscent of [i][gameid=54][/i].  The shape of the pieces themselves, stacking and aligning land types feels like [i][gameid=855][/i].  Growing the play area organically without a formal game board has been lifted from Merkle's own excellent game, [i][gameid=8051][/i].

The production value of this game is beyond [i][gameid=855][/i]-esque.  The tiles in [i]Taluva[/i] are [i]thicker,[/i] something I would not have thought possible.  The rules are simple if you're learning them from someone who already knows the game, otherwise they are missing a couple of small but critical clarifications.  A few pointers: any time you want to expand a settlement to a higher level, you [i]must[/i] have the prerequisite number of huts for that new level.  You can never have a single hut on a level two space, for example.  You can place a Temple (or a Tower) as the linking piece between two settlements, even if one of them already has a Temple (or a Tower, if you're placing a Tower) in it: select the [i]other[/i] Settlement as the one for which you are placing the Temple, and ignore all others.  Minus one point for these oversights, as they unnecessarily increase the learning curve.

With enough patience, the game comes together beautifully. The artwork is attractive, and a completed game looks fantastic on the table.  After a couple of learning sessions to familiarize myself with the rules, I had an "aha!" moment where they all came together.  I realized how simple the game really is.  There are plenty of opportunities for players to interfere with each other, especially since there is no penalty for playing in a different area of the board -- unlike [i][gameid=8051][/i], where a new settlement requires extra investment. The two-player game is more strategic simply because there are fewer players to get in the way.  The three-player game requires a more defensive mindset, and there can be [i][gameid=8051][/i]-like experiences where one player is forced to stop the opponent to her left.  I haven't played with four players, but I imagine that the game becomes even more chaotic.

[i]Taluva[/i] is a strong game in its own right even though you might feel as though you've played it before.  Some portions of it are recycled, but they haven't all been brought together this elegantly.  I'm looking forward to my next game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
I've taught this game to a number of people and at first this game's rules seem overwhelming.  What I've learned at least is that it's best with 2 players, especially if you are trying to play for your first time.  There are a lot of little tricks to the placement of the game-board pieces for protecting your huts that I didn't understand at first, but now I find myself watching my opponent's strategy to see whether I want to crush them or just help my own settlements. There is a deep feeling of satisfaction that comes with crushing your opponents' pieces to be sure, but you always have to keep in mind the game objective is to get rid of 2 sets of your pieces not only to smash!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[A friend gave me his copy.]
This game really surprised and impressed me. I didn't expect to like it as much as I did. Having just played Java (another 3D game), I wasn't looking forward to it that much (I like Java, but I wouldn't want to play it every week). But Taluva has a very different feel while retaining that cool aspect of building out and up to create an awesome-looking board by the end. Unlike Java, the game goes very quickly, because players don't get stuck trying to calculate out seventeen different possibilities. You only get two moves--place a tile and place a wooden piece--so there are fewer choices. I also liked that even with four players, it was still slightly strategic. True, several times someone took the spot that I was gonna place my piece in, but I was able to grab and build up a couple of areas before someone else interfered. With four players, Java is pretty much just tactical. With instruction, Taluva took 30 minutes, and I was totally ready to play again as soon as it ended. It's an excellent, quick-playing, meaty filler. I expect that my rating will increase from the initial 8 after repeated plays.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Beautifully elegant and works great with 2 or 3, perhaps a little chaotic with 4
The key rule is that if you can't build, you lose, which constrains your other building options elegantly and makes for a tense battle that often leaves all players within a move or so of winning at the end. Three player is great since effective  ganging up on the leader needs intelligent cooperation between the other parties, neatly avoiding the Attika "It's your problem" issues.
And afterward; a game where you can stand back and admire the visual glory of the finished board.
UPDATE: Increased rating to 9, still finding more neat twists. Strategic placement of the tiles to set up or prevent village self-destruction keeps the game endlessly fascinating. The more we play, the more often towers take on a role since the fast temples-and-huts win gets blocked. Often see wins with forks these days, you can block my last temple but only by giving me space to build my last tower....

RATING: 8
A bit abstract -- the game is virtually randomness- and luck-free -- but neat, and really, really quick.  It fits a similar niche as Hey! That's My Fish!, but this is more beautiful and has much less setup time.  (Not as good, in my opinion, though.)  My wife doesn't like it, because it's hard to see a good move on your turn.  It's not a casual game, at least with 2 players.

It is a racing game of sorts, in which you're trying to make each move as efficient as possible.  Games will usually be [i]very[/i] close.

I've played with 2, 3 and 4.  Some people think it is an excellent two-player game, but I prefer it with more, definitely.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Recently, I have begun to put much more emphasis on the designer of a game, because much experience has shown that certain designers either make games that appeal to me or for whatever reason hold a better grasp of what makes a good game.  With respect to Merkle, I remained dubious before playing Taluva.  Verrater and Meuterer rank high amongst the games where my opinion differs vastly from that of the BGG populace.  These two games suck.  However, despite some minor blemishes I really do adore Attika.

Well, Taluva is not only Merkle's best game, but a great game by any standard.  I would put it up as the epitome of German design goals as it packs a lot of game into very simple rules and a very short duration.  Amazingly, the game boasts very strong tactical tile placement and a bit of strategy in deciding which buildings to puruse.  Also, due to some smart design decisions, it avoids the usual problem of analysis paralysis usually associated with tile-laying games of this type.  Yet for all its excellence in game design, it doesn't neglect more visceral "fun" elements such as crushing people's huts with a volcano.

Highly recommended to everyone.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
Nice abstract tile layer. The rules are simple but the  rulebook is awful, so after a bad start and a few queries on BGG we had to scrap our first game and start over. Once we got the hang of it, it started to get a puzzly feel in the way that many abstracts have. You spend much of your turn thinking about where to place, trying to make tile configurations that your opponent cannot break up while also fulfilling one of the victory conditions. Bumping people's huts doesn't hurt them nearly as much as you might think it does, and you can quickly dig yourself into a hole if you use up too many of your own huts. A lot of room for growth and mastery.

RATING: 9
A (totally awesome) gift from my 2012 BGG Secret Santa.  Getting an expensive, out-of-print game is really going above and beyond the call.

This is probably my favorite 2-player abstract.  The tile draw provides just enough randomness that you don't feel compelled to plan too far ahead, but not so much that you feel it determines the game.  It's simple to teach and quick to play, while offering plenty of strategic decisions.  And it looks super cool.  What more could you want?

The box says it plays with 2-4, but I think of it exclusively as a 2-player game.  With more players, it's too chaotic.

RATING: 8
An excellent family game! There is quite a bit of room for messing with your opponents, but the spirit of the game is not as mean as in some other games of this type. Take notice when someone else is about to build a temple and try to stop it with clever tile or hut placement. That is, unless you immediately need to set yourself up to build your own temple. In that case, let some other player take care of it! Also, watch where you place your tiles, because you might be able to use a volcano placement to split a settlement into two parts to prepare to add another temple. When playing defensively, it's most important to concentrate on the player who goes immediately after you, because no one else will have a chance to limit their effectiveness. Conversely, if you are currently playing for your own gain, expanding settlements, try to not leave the player immediately after you with a really advantageous opportunity.

RATING: 9
This has grown on me with repeated play. It's beautiful once set up, it's simple, it's got some interesting tactics. It's a bit of a trashing game, in that you have plenty of opportunity to slow down and interfere with your opponents, but the trashing is subtle enough, and requires enough cooperation, that it doesn't seem to suffer as horribly as the designer's previous game, Attika, in which keeping the player on your left from winning was an overriding objective in the game. Here, you're trading off the need to get ahead with the need to cooperate with other players to slow down a leader in much more interesting ways.

I think the game is simple and clever enough that the tremendously well-done presentation and decent theme for this category of games kicks it up to an 9 for me, and it's almost certainly a keeper.

RATING: 9
Wow - what an excellent and unique game this is! Never played anything like it, and the first time I played it, we played it 3 times in a row which is very unusual for my group! I have played it quite a lot compared to many other games. However, somehow it seems that people are torn between this game; some love it, others really don't care for it. I'm definitely in the first group.

Regarding the strategy, I need to say that it does seem that it's easier to win by placing and getting rid of your towers and huts compared to the temples, but that might just be because of how we play it. Also, I mainly play Taluva with 3 players total, so the tiles don't deplete and we always end up winning in the premature way. With 4 players, the stack of tiles might run out, and then the winner is determined on the number of temples, so temples are much more important with 4 players! In order to make it a more tense game for 2-3 players, you can remove some of the tiles so you can run out of tiles and find a winner under the normal victory conditions, and I guess the towers aren't the best then. We like to keep all of the tiles in our game, though, so towers win most of the times. Only a few times have I seen a game being won by placing all your temples and towers, but it's very rare.

Also, it's a pretty big advantage to be the starting player as the game can be over immediately when a player only has one type of buildings left; if you start, you kind of have an extra turn as you're closer to getting rid of your buildings, and I've seen in a few of my games that other players could win in the same turn as the starting player did, so that's a little imbalanced. 

Still, it's a great, fast and tactical game and is a good choice to play for both new players and true gamers as a light "filler" which can be played in 20 minutes if you know the game - we are actually finishing most of our 3p games in 15 minutes, sometimes even less.

Taluva is gorgeous to look at when you are done, and I really recommend this game! The only downside of this game is that it's OOP at the moment, but if you can find one, even if it's expensive, buy it!

RATING: 7
Let's start this with an "8" rating, though it might go up or down with more plays. It's hard not to compare/contrast this with Carcassonne. Carcassonne is light, random, simple, and playful (unless you really play cutthroat). Taluva is tactical, thinky, and a bit obtuse. There's some complexity in remembering the restrictions to each building type, and the pros and cons regarding where to play tiles is not immediately obvious. But unlike Carcassonne, there's a lot going on here and it offers greater depth.

One thing for sure, the game looks gorgeous as you lay tiles, stack them up, and create an island.

Update: surprisingly, I traded this game away. It's a good game, and looks gorgeous on the table. It's just a tad too abstract and simply wasn't getting played with any real frequency. Given the fact it is not widely available, I figured it was good trade bait.

RATING: 7
What a pleasant surprise. This game has a unique feel and has been extremely well-received by all the local players I know who've played it.

Firstly, I love the physical components. Very Java-like tiles, nice and thick. 

I also like that there is no scoring or VPs to calculate. There are simply winning conditions. Also, per the rules, you can be a "premature winner". That kicks ass!

The rules are straightforward, but the decision-making is rich. Also, this game doesn't FEEL like its obviously derivative of any other game. It has its  own unique niche.

The weak point would be that it has the Puerto Rico syndrome...where a weak player can unintentionally throw the game to the player after him/her in turn order. There is not mechanism to mitigate that phenomenon. But with players of comparable strength, its a good contest.

RATING: 3
When I win a game, I like to feel like it was because I had a good strategy, I was really lucky, or a combination of both.  In my first game of TALUVA, I won and I had absolutely no grasp of strategy, placement tactics, and I was hazy on one or two of the critical rules.  Talk about unsatisfying.  TALUVA is a reasonable abstract game without a lick of theme despite a handsome production that plays sort of like JAVA stripped of A LOT of fat and without those stupid, pointless festivals. As a simple, tile-laying/placement game TALUVA is moderately successful as long as it doesn't break the 30 minute time barrier.  There are a few opportunities for destructive, confrontational play that can swing the game one way or another but mostly it's a pretty light affair.  I don't hate the game, but I don't feel terribly compelled to play it again either.  Fans of ATTIKA, JAVA, FJORDS, and those types of games will probably get at least a couple of games out of it before the realization of the game's shallowness and repetition sets in.

RATING: 7
Fun tile-laying game with beautiful bits which add considerably to the game. The fact that you can grow your little island upwards gives it extra appeal. However, I'm not so sure yet about the game play. Originally I envisioned something of a Rat Hot, which can become really, really mean at times. Taluva is far tamer than that, as huts already placed already count for you. The only way to hinder someone then is by cutting short the size of their settlements so they can't build temples. But as I haven't seen a victory by placing all tiles, I fear the temples are rather redundant to begin with, and are just useful to threaten with. As it is, there isn't much 'game' in there.

I think I'm going to fiddle with house rules in order to emphasize nasty play. The game's rating is an undeserved 6, and should go up to a 7 once I'm done with it.

[b]Update[/b] As it turned out, the simplest way of fixing matters is also the way present in later editions of the game: simply remove a number of tiles prior to play. I'm not sure I agree with the 12 tiles per player-formula in those later editions; this seems to curtail the other victory condition too much because play turns a lot more agressive. I'm going for a 14-13-12 variant for 2-3-4 players respectively. Upped the rate too.

RATING: 8
What a delightfully simple and elegant game.  This was easy to learn, and the complexity of strategy revealed itself quickly so I could see plenty of replay potential.

The board builds out and up with thick chunky tiles as players try to expand their settlements with huts, and set up a good arrangement to add temples (for expanding outward) or towers (for expanding upward).  Blocking and destroying your opponents settlements are key to victory.

Beautifully made, plays fast, and works perfectly for 2 players.  Really glad to have this added to my collection.

I liked that the game end is triggered by the players and not some random timer. Victory is also simple - it's about how many village pieces you place. RATING: 7.5
With it's take a tile, place a tile, place a marker (a settlement in this case, rather than a worker) mechanism, the comparison to Carcassonne is inevitable. However, I do think that Taluva occupies a different niche: it's quicker and less involved than Carcassonne  but for a half hour game it is very involving and the two possible end / victory conditions (which in the games I've played come up roughly equally often) make for some interesting tactical choices.

Also, in terms of visuals and tactility - it's one of the finest games I've played.

Perfect as a quick filler or as an introduction for new gamers.

RATING: 6
Nicely presented tile laying and placement game.  Players on their turn draw and place a landscape tile, which can expand an ever growing island laterally, or start growing volcanoes vertically.  After placing a tile, the player takes one of four building placement options - placing huts, temples or towers.  The game is over when all tiles are used up, or when one player has completely placed two or their three types of pieces.  The game is fine, simple to pick up and play, but super bland.  Good for a few plays before trading it on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
What a great, neat little game.  The pieces are gorgeous, and the mechanics are quite interesting.  It's definitely an abstract strategy game though, don't expect much theme.  I really like the way the board develops over time, and the mechanics are simple to learn, but offer a fair bit of depth.  I felt as I played my first game, new insights were gradually coming to me in how I could better position my pieces.  Also, I love that the ending can be triggered in different ways, which can sometimes sneak up on you, and can reward smart tactical play with a nice surprise victory.  Looking forward to future plays.

RATING: 6
Taluva is a good tile laying game with a lot of player interaction.  I feel that it will scale well regardless of the number of players but more than two will add some chaos to the game.  I consider it to be a good gateway game because there aren't a lot of rules and the play time is short to allow for multiple games.  I think Attika is the more popular game by this designer but I prefer this one because the game feels less random.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A beautiful game in terms of presentation and game material. Building the ever-growing mountain world works great and allows for many interesting placement decisions (as well as looking really great). The rules are simple and quickly explained. Where the game falls down a little for me is that it inevitably runs down to a kingmaking finale if played with equally competent players. In our game it ran down to this: Player A couldn't win anymore, but could prevent player B from winning, which he did. Player B could now not win either, but could make a decision that either could make player C or player D win. It lies in the nature of this game that situations like this WILL occur, and one thinks if there couldn't have been some other design decisions to prevent this. With part-time gamers this problem won't be as evident, though, and this game will certainly be enjoyed if played in a family setting, as it isn't too difficult to grasp.

RATING: 4
[6 plays] I think my judgment on Taluva is that I don't like it because I don't enjoy the experience of playing; I don't like the way the game makes me feel. Even when I won, which I sometimes did, I didn't enjoy it. 

We played, as often in Carcassonne, with drawing a tile at the end of your turn, so that you have until your turn comes around again to think about where to put it. Whatever plan you come up with, though, will inevitably be blighted by some action taken by another player. If it doesn't happen till the person right before you, then you will be sitting there taking time to re-plan. Or you can make multiple plans--I'll do this, or if this gets blocked I'll do that, or if this and that both get blocked I'll do something else--but it doesn't help much; either they all get blocked, or there's such a multiplicity of them in your head that you can no longer recall which was your favorite. 

In addition to "I was going to go here and start this great thing, but he blocked it," there's also a lot of "I started to build this thing, but couldn't finish it before someone interfered with me." So it's a constant cycle, or rather a plethora of constantly overlapping cycles, of having your plans crushed and thwarted. And you have to watch this play out in relatively slow motion.

After all, such a brilliant game must have an amazing designer who has developed OTHER brilliant games right? Taluva stood out to me [the artwork and production quality is excellent] and after a year of seeing if I could find it and being unsuccessful, I ended up ordering it from Italy. I needn't have bothered. It is something that just passes the time. Maybe it's better with more players? I am not usually bored by games because I love the game-playing experience, but this one really was boring. No tension, no excitement, nothing particularly interesting happened...no thanks!

RATING: 6
Gorgeous components, and pretty simple rules.  There are many subtleties to the design that are easy to forget -- you can only start a new settlement at ground level, for example, which (intentionally) greatly limits your ability to block opponents going for towers if you're not already in the neighborhood.  The tile draw *usually* doesn't matter, but sometimes helps or hinders your ability to expand settlements.

JAN 2012: I have been trying to trade for a copy of this for 3 years. I owned Java (twice) but heard so much about Taluva being similar (and shorter) that when Java went up in value, I thought I'd get rid of Java and acquire Taluva. Good thing I played it first. So now I have neither, which is probably for the best: as good as Java is, it doesn't seem to go over well with anyone I teach it too. 

JUN 2014: My opinion two years later is the same, but worse. Taluva is SO dry and simple. It's missing the story arc that Java provides, but the reduction in time isn't worth the trade-off for me. Downgrading from 6 to 5.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Almost without fail, the first time someone plays this they have no idea what the goal is, or where there's any place to even begin to develop a strategy, so I can see where some reviewers are coming from thinking it's pointless or overly abstract.

That being said, after the first game, once you have the game mechanics down and you can realize how they factor into the progression of the game and how you can use them against your opponents things get fun I think. A strategy will start to develop; I'm not going to lie and say this is the most thought-provoking game in the world, so it may be a pretty simple strategy, but there is strategy in this game. Utilizing different terrain types, planning the landscape to use different building types, blocking opponents moves, etc.

In relative terms, compared to the greats this is probably more like a 6.5 or 7, but I bump it up to 8 because it's: casual, fun, really nice to look at, a great two player game, and got a high replayablity factor.

RATING: 7
The tile stacking mechanism feels different than in other similar games. It works really well. The rest of the game is interesting and the winning conditions makes it possible to go for various strategies. An impressive feat is that every ending condition seems to occur almost simultaneously (update: this is only true for a 4 player game, unfortunately with less player, the sudden death is almost the only way to win). The minor drawback is that, as they are, the rule favor the first players a little bit too much since a game can end prematurely, giving one extra move to some players. This can easily be corrected with an home-made variant though (ie. complete the current turn...)

RATING: 5
If you want to compare Taluva to other games, Java is likely the obvious choice simply because of the three-dimensional hex playing field. If you go beyond the (great) looks, however, it feels much closer to Carcassonne, albeit quite a bit more abstract.

While the game does have opportunities for clever tactical play, winning or losing seems to depend a lot on how much the other players are willing to invest in destructive play. And that's not a good thing, in most cases.

RATING: 8
Taluva is definitely one of the stranger tile-placement games I've played.  The rules are simple, and yet strategies aren't revealed until you play a couple of times.  After that, the subtlety of the game comes through, and you begin to see that your move might have more repercussions than believed.  Taluva is quick to play (it feels like a race, somewhat) and there is always motivation to play another time.  Apart from play, the components are beautiful.  The wood is artistically designed, and the tiles are the thickest you'll ever see in a game.
It is perfect for 2 players as well.  Excellent!

RATING: 8
Taluva is a very pretty game - nice artwork and stylish pieces.  It also plays well for a strategic game.  As two player there isn't too much that is random about it, but still has many options to try and forward your cause (getting the most temples, or other instant victory conditions). =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
It's a beautiful and quick playing abstract/tile-laying hybrid with a lot of interaction. The randomness of the tiles is the only thing that prevents this from being a pure abstract but as I don't usually like abstracts, I like that randomness. The rotation and placement of the tiles end up mattering more anyway. The tiles are beautifully illustrated and the 3D landscape that you end up with in the end is wonderful.

The gameplay has very simple rules, but there's plenty of opportunity for smart plays and counterplays. The 2 player game feels like a power struggle and it works very well. It can be hard to grok the 3D and volcanos and it can be cruel at times, but still a lot if fun!

RATING: 5
Very chess like. I have to admit that I get tired playing games where I must think 4-5 moves ahead in order to play well. You have to keep a constant watch over the board so that you will: 
     1) see and be able to stop your opponent's obviously good moves (sometimes this means seeing 2-3 steps ahead, because it is very easy to notice an opponents play after it is too late to stop it)
     2) not make a play that will help your opponent as much or more than you. These types of plays are very easy as your opponent can make just as much use of your tile placements as you. 
This is definitely a game where even the most basic strategy will likely ellude you until you have a handful of games under your belt. Has hit the trade list in search of a game that will see more plays.

RATING: 7
Taluva is the creation of Marcel-Andre Casasola Merkle, designer of such games as Attika, Meuterer and Verrater.  It is essentially a tile-laying game, but tiles can be stacked, creating a landscape that expands both horizontally and vertically.  The challenge is creating and noticing placement opportunities that will allow for the placement of huts, towers and temples.  

The game does require a certain amount of visualization skills, as players must be able to discern favorable placement opportunities and work towards long-term goals.  These plans can be upset by one’s opponents, but part of the challenge is in overcoming these obstacles.  In these respects, Taulva reminds me a bit of a simplified version of Java, one of Wolfgang Kramer’s excellent designs.  It is easier to play than Java, but it still requires considerable skill and foresight to play well.

Taluva packs quite a bit of skill, tension and strategy in less than an hour of playing time, and seems to play equally as well with three or four players.  Games develop differently each time I’ve played, which keeps it fresh and challenging.  While the game is accessible to all skill levels, experience counts, and it truly shines when played with a group of seasoned players.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Excellent game, with simple, elegant and streamlined rules. It does borrow a lot from "Java" (which I also enjoy very much), but it's much faster and more family-friendly, still keeping good tactical depth. It's equally important to position tiles and your pieces. Likewise, hindering other players is as important as improving your position. Two-player games can (or must) be very confrontational. I can see the potential for kingmaking with more players, but otherwise it scales very well and is an almost perfect design. Being very beautiful doesn't hurt, of course.

RATING: 7
This game was recommended to me in a forum thread where I asked for games with deep interesting strategy that played in less than an hour.  Several of the games from that thread I have tried were duds - Taluva most definitely is not!  This fast playing abstract has beautiful pieces, and a number of tense decision points.  Your opponents are trying to get rid of their pieces and trying to screw each other at the same time.  You aren't just trying to drop as many pieces as possible though, you are trying to set yourself up for success in future turns while limiting your opponent's success through your choices.  Its fast and deep.  The downside is its themeless and can cause some analysis paralysis as people consider what everyone else might do.  This is Carcasonne for people who aren't handicapped.  Its totally abstract, but a very deep and interesting game!

RATING: 7
This is a beautiful abstract game that plays in 3 dimensions. I can't remember what I traded this away for, but I regret it.

Update (August 2014): Played it again a few years after having traded it away. I still think that the game is absolutely stunning, simple, and quite quirky and clever. It certainly isn't the most intuitive game, but that makes it somewhat interesting. By this, I mean that the way in which the game plays out is not so transparent, causal, and tactically separable (e.g. a move that immediately hurts your opponent might be beneficial to them in the long run or moves that benefit you might not only benefit you). This adds a particular flair to the game in the same way that a game like TZAAR might -- it's not clear what the best, or even what a good move might immediately be, and that is, in my opinion, part of the mystique of this game.

Update (november 2014): I recently acquired a second copy of Taluva. This, when playing 2 players, allows for the possibility of not only double or triple Taluva, but QUADRUPLE Taluva. This turns a largely tactical game into a largely tactical game with a larger strategic horizon (which is a welcome change). Not to mention that the large resultant island is absolutely gorgeous and far more diverse from game to game. Upgrading rating from 7 to 8.

RATING: 7.25
Taluva is a solid, appealing abstract with beautiful pieces that have the look and feel of quality.

It's a very nice, quick-playing game with a tropical island theme, and supports up to four players.

The game is played on a three-dimensional island, built of extra-heavy three-hex star tiles by the players during their play. Each depicts a volcano, plus two other island terrains, sometimes matching but more often not.

Each turn players take and place a tile, then play one or more buildings onto the board.  The tiles can be piled, by putting the volcano portion of the tile on top of another, but in a different orientation, so tiles are completely supported but do not pile directly atop each other.

Because of the random tile pull/play, the board is basically unique every play, and the 3D effect can be very interesting for strategy.

Players try to run out of two sets of buildings; however, if they cannot play a building legally, then they are out of the game entirely.

Of course the timing and pre-planning of placements can be thrown off somewhat by the random tile draw, or by opponents' moves, which keeps the game fresh.

I've rated this a 7, but I am tempted to increase it, because I think it's really a very good game, and the board builds to an attractive and beautiful structure, with the wooden buildings and the piled volcanos.

This was out of print for several years, but like Merkle's game Attika this is a really terrific themed abstract, and I'm glad to see it available again for others to enjoy.

RATING: 8
Fascinating variant of the Carcassone mechanic of 1) draw a tile, 2) play it, and 3) play your meeples. Except in this one, the tiles are tri-hexagonal, and tiles can be placed to bury your opponents in lava! The only problem is that the early part of the game is trying to determine the best course of play, as there is no way to foresee the future; you just react to the tile you just drew, trying to maximize the benefits. Still, the components are gorgeous, with the super-thick tiles a pleasure to play, and stack, sort of like in Java (which I wish I could find a copy of). Seems fairly random, but eventually, long-term plays can be setup with deliberate tile placement. All in all a fun, fast, simple game, that belittles the deep tactical and strategic play that lies beneath its pretty exterior.

Update (10/31/09) 4 plays: Played this twice in rapid succession one evening. I'm still befuddled by the lack of any long-term strategy that is apparent; the game is mostly multi-player solitaire, with some interaction of the "you screw me, I screw you" variety. It is also somewhat dependent on what tile you happen to draw this round. Perhaps this game doesn't work with 3 players. I like it, but am beginning to wonder about the "strategy"--there's plenty of tactics, no doubt.

Update (08/09/10) 6 plays: The game is rather chaotic and random in the beginning, as each player does his/her own thing, but then becomes a tactical game of move/counter-move when play-sequences become apparent. It still hasn't gotten any traction in my gaming group.

RATING: 8.5
A very clever abstract strategy game. Success demands planning ahead, but at times it makes more sense to interrupt your own plans to interfere with another player. Stopping a player from placing a temple by crushing part of their settlement can lead to enough "high ground" to wind up giving them a tower instead. Subtle, very strategic, very satisfying to see a plan come together for an unstoppable win. Expect to play it a few times before you realize all of the implications of what you and your opponents are doing. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.65
[b]2-3 Player Review:[/b]

This is my go-to drinking game. Whenever just drinking a few Kirin Beer, shooting the breeze, and chilling, Taluva will hit the table. The speed of play, ease of rules yet surprising level of strategy, and organic nature of the game board, make it a hit every time. 

There isn't a massive level of difference between 2 and 3 player counts, except for a slight amount more randomness. And after introducing the new end game condition for tied games ...keep on building... the tension and out enjoyment has increased event more so. 

It's my favorite gateway game but leaps and bounds. 

[b]Pros:[/b]

- The artwork and pieces are beautiful
- I can't help but take a photo of every island once the game is complete. They always look so interesting and cool. 
- The strategy is intense, especially if you resolve tied games by continuing to build until someone can't make a legal move. 
- Very quick and easy to teach. I've never had someone new to board games not enjoy themselves on the very first few turns. 
- Plays very quickly.

[b]Cons:[/b]

- The great game-play and simple but smart rule set makes me wish there were more building types. 
- Some advanced terrain types that add add to the strategy would be super sweet too!

[b]2015 Rating:[/b] 9.65 [b]Rank:[/b] 6 [b]Plays:[/b] 33
[b]2014 Rating:[/b] 9.00 [b]Rank:[/b] - [b]Plays:[/b] 29
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
  Played first time with 2P, 3 games in a row. The first game will be shaky with some rules questions and no strategy.  If you quit after one game, it will just seem like a random placement game. 2nd game will go smoother, and by the 3rd one you are no longer discussing options with your opponent, you are trying to lava them.  Some say there is a kingmaker problem, geez, the game is 15-20 minutes long, but yes, if you make a bad move, you can find yourself trying to catch up, but there is the chance your opponent will miss something and then he is behind.  Either way, the game plays fast so I don't see kingmaking as an issue such as it would be in a longer, drawn out game.   As for abstract, eh, maybe, I HATED Clans and Samurai, but this is nothing like them.  Can't compare to Tikal, Java or Attika, but it does feel a little like Carcassonne, but much deeper.  I really enjoy it and want to play again, and so does my non-geek husband.  Plus, the scoring is soooo easy, no points to count, no tokens, easy! LOVE it.

[b]Favorite game of 2006[/b]

:thumbsup: aesthetically archipelagic
:thumbsup: inimically interactive
:thumbsup: pithy playtime

:thumbsdown: mechanically unintuitive
:thumbsdown: 3+players makes for a more chaotic experience, Analysis Paralysis is exacerbated with each additional player, and inexperienced opponents can inadvertently king-make.

A deep, tactically opaque, abstract race that requires simultaneous offensive and defensive play, while thinking ahead a couple of moves in multiple dimensions to create forks of opportunity, and a little bit of variance to prevent the game from ever being solved.

RATING: 7
Very short, light game, with deceptively challenging tactics and some amusing moments.  You are natives living on a volcanic isle.  Each turn you place a tile and then place one or more villager pieces according to certain rules.  You win if you get two of them entirely out first or if you get more out then everyone else when the game ends.  Best part is when you place a tile on top of other tiles to form an volcanic eruption, squishing another player's villagers.  :)

RATING: 7
So far I am very impressed with this confrontational tile-placement game. The game is vaguely reminiscent of Carcassonne (place a tile then add "meeples"), but with room for a lot more 'direct' nastiness. The connection to one of the designer's other games, Attika, is also there: Players build towards the final victory condition, but all the time have to keep an eye out for sudden death victories.

I am not sure how stable my rating is yet: It feels like Taluva is the sort of game I could easily tire of (or where, at least, I could tire of the nastiness involved). Until then, it's a solid '8'.

RATING: 7
An interesting game.  There doesn't seem to be a lot going on... I mean, 'play a tile, add a piece (or pieces)...'

But, after a couple of plays, there is definitely a lot of strategy.  Do you attack a neighbour to try and force him to continually expand with two or more huts, forcing him to expend his buildings faster?  Do you scatter a number of single huts all over the board, knowing that they cannot be covered, then expand them all for a temple win?  Coax an opponent to build a level 3 tile next to one of your settlements, knowing that you and he are the only ones who can build towers?

Aggressive play appears to be rewarded.

Lots going on for such a simple game...  a definite winner.  Best of all... it plays in about 40 minutes with 4 players so it should see the table a lot this year.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
For as light as this game is mechanically, it plays very tight and a single mis-played tile can cost you the game. Attractive, well-made, and fast, this game should have a pretty wide appeal. It's a good design. The box, though, is an unjustified space hog!

Although folks say this is a great game for two, I think the game is ok for two but plays FAR better with 3 or 4. I think a big part of the appeal for me is the beauty of the multi-tiered landscape that evolves. It's really gorgeous.

RATING: 6
Taluva es un juego abstracto sobre construir una civilizacion en una isla volcanica.

En cada turno se coloca una loseta y una o varias figuras. Con las losetas se trata de abrir un camino para tus figuras, o cerrarlo para las figuras de tus rivales. También puedes eliminar figuras de tus rivales con las losetas. Una vez colocada la loseta se ponen las figuras que se puedan.

Buenos materiales, y además queda muy chulo.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2011
[/color][color=blue]
Taluva is a good abstract game about building a civilization along a volcanic island.

Each round, you will have to put a tile and one or some figures. Using the tiles, you will try to open a way for your figures or to close an opponent's way. You can also eliminate your opponent's figures with the tiles. Once you have played your tile, you will have to put one or more figures.

It has very good components, and it also looks so cool.[/color]

RATING: 7
There are few games which look better at the end of the game. Each game will create a completely different world of tiles and pieces. But there's also a game in there that is much deeper than the simple Carcassonne-like turn mechanism of first placing a cardboard tile and then placing a meeple suggests. Actual gameplay is more akin to a multiplayer abstract than Carcassonne. It is essential to plan your position a few turns ahead while not neglecting to thwart the preparations of your opponents. Our results were so close that there is a potential for kingmaking. Could this be avoided with more experience?

RATING: 7
Una obra de arte, señores.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In short - Carcassone 3D. Similar tile draw and placement, but in this game there is no requirement to match one tile with others. Similar building (meeple in Carcassone) placement, only here there is no need to take them back. It is interesting that even buildings, that are removed from the game, are also counted for final scoring. However the most interesting aspect - map building upwards. Because of this you must plan where to place the tiles in order not to make too much holes. It is not hard to place the second level tile, but much harder to place the third one.

Trumpai - Carcassone 3D. Panašus kortelių traukimas ir dėliojimas, tik tiek, kad nereikia sutaikyti ką prie ko dėti. Panašiai ant kortelių reikia statyti pastatus (žmogeliukus Carcassonėje), tik tiek, kad čia nebūtina (o ir nereikia) jų atsiimti. Įdomu tai, kad net gi iš žemėlapio pašalinami namukai skaitosi skaičiuojant taškus. Na bet įdomiausias aspektas - kad žemėlapį galima statyti į aukštį. Todėl reikia tokius dalykus planuoti ir neprisidaryti per daug skylių. Antrą lygi pastatyti nėra sunku, bet trečią jau kur kas sunkiau.

RATING: 9
Mr Casasola Merkle has made a game that has a clear reminiscence to his former hit Attika, but these two games are still considerably different from each other to maintain the interest in both. Especially the difference between the two is huge in the looks. The box cover of Taluva is horrible, but what's inside looks really, really good. With Attika these are quite the opposite. Componentwise this may be the best game I've played. Mechanically this also works quite beautifully and probability of possible player elimination seems to be exceptionally low, which is just good. Two routes to victory is also good, even though the ways are not so far away from each other as in Attika and the so called regular path to victory seems to be not so usual. And just as with Attika rules are almost simple and really clear.

RATING: N/A
In Taluva, players attempt to successfully settle a South Sea island slowly emerging from the ocean waters as volcano after volcano erupts.

Each turn, players decide to either have a new volcano erupt along the shore, increasing the size of the island, or to have an existing volcano erupt again, increasing the height of the land around it (and possibly destroying parts of existing settlements). They do this by placing a new tile, consisting of one volcano and two other types of landscape. A tile must always touch at least one other tile, when placed at sea level, or be placed on top of at least two other tiles (without any gaps under the land being created), with the volcano being placed on top of an existing volcano.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[2007.Mar.02] In essence, this is a 3-dimensional, tile-laying game by veteran game designer Marcel-André Casasola Merkle. The rules are simple: (1) draw & place a new tile on the gaming surface, and (2) build (at least) one building on one of the tiles. One of the unexpected aspects of this Euro game is that players can be eliminated (which is an unusual trait for a Euro). However, this only happens if a player truly mismanages his/her settlement resources (i.e., buildings). The Rio Grande Games publication is quite appealing with elegant, wooden building pieces (3 different types) and game tiles reminiscent of Java's thick, meaty tiles.

RATING: 7
4 plays.

Nice 3D tile game.  Beautiful bits.

Destroying your opponents huts can give great feelings to those who like confrontation in games.

The rules for settlement placement are somewhat contrived, which forces you to re-read the rules if you are not accustomed enough to them.

Suffers from the "Kill Dr Lucky" syndrome, where you might have to sacrifice yourself so that the leading player does not win immediately; that's something your other opponents will appreciate.  So, it's best to play with players of a similar mastering level--or don't have a good player sit to your right.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Initial rating after 3.5 plays (the 0.5 is from my practice game with myself, which I lost). The big confusing part of explaining this game has to do with the temples and towers. The alliteration of these playing pieces makes them difficult to distinguish the rules for each. Once past this hurdle, however, the game is a fun abstract of blocking and island building.

[update1] Rating now a 9. The rules are simple but there is a lot of depth here that isn't immediately apparent.

[update2] Rating back down to 8. It turns out we were playing with the wrong rules. I thought the "expand" option meant you had to expand all connecting hexes of the same landscape. Also we missed that you cannot destroy a settlement. This slows down the game considerably and can limit tile-laying options. Do I sense some house rules in the works?

[u]Final[/u]: Traded because wifey did not like so was not getting played.

RATING: 7.5
While the artwork is very nice, this is really an abstract tile laying game with a very loose theme. Several ways of winning mean that there is a variety of strategies. There is a small amount of luck involved in the tile draw, which abstract purists won't like, but it also means that the game never feels totally lopsided in favour of the better player.

The components are top-notch. The wooden pieces are attractive, and the cardboard tiles are very thick and sturdy. Again, abstract purists will probably lament the absence of bakelite (which is indeed a lovely material), but there's nothing to fault here.

RATING: 7
A very nice tile based game.  Players build a three dimensional island on which they have to build settlements comprising huts, towers and temples.  There are restrictions on how buildings can be placed and the terrain can be changed to accommodate or confound placement.  Players must always be able to place or they're out of the game.  Lots of nice little strategies to use.  The biggest weakness is that, particularly in 3 player games where open discussion is allowed, one player can easily become kingmaker, which is always unsatisfying. Probably better with even number of players.

RATING: 7.78
First grail game. Bought an imported copy since Funagain has a monopoly on US distribution and currently has it listed as being $10 more than an important version. Seriously.

Game Quality: A solid multiplayer abstract that could almost fall into the filler area. Certainly a two-player game using 24 tiles can be cranked out in 15 or so minutes. Rulebook is four pages and that's being kind. There's a low amount of interaction but just enough to where it isn't that vicious of a game. The components are really stellar though. Random factor will go up as there are more players so people need to decide where they want that to ultimately fall. 7.

Would I buy this again if I was starting over: If I was hell bent set on just getting one multiplayer abstract Samurai would likely win out if I ultimately wanted to classify it as an abstract, but I don't. Samurai gives a feel of being the meatier game and those tend to win out for me. I still think I would. Due to various issues, I fear this is becoming not worth the cost in terms of bang for buck. Granted, as I write this Samurai isn't either due to its current OOP status. If this were priced reasonably, then absolutely. 7.

Do I want to play this if asked: There's not a ton to mine from the game as it is a rather straight forward with little options type of game, but it is enjoyable for now and has the advantage of being easy, quick, and visually appealing enough to where I feel like it fills a role. Splendor is another lighter multiplayer abstract that is solid, but this feels like the decision making process is more open. 8

Overall: 7.78.

Rating Potential: The more I play it, the more I like it. Might be limited in how high it goes since for now I view Samurai as the better game, but there's not much separation.

RATING: 3
A hearty "Meh" for this one. The artwork is nice, I can see why an abstract fan might get into this, but it's not my thing. It just doesn't draw me into the action or immerse me in the theme, and it's more or less a puzzle that (like most puzzles) intrigues me for a minute or two then fades quickly.
I'm never quite sure whether to nod in appreciation or shake my head in bemusement at the way Euros lamely attempt to paste a theme on to games that really are just abstract, competitive puzzles. Do Euro fans really need these (usually) pasted-on themes to enjoy the games? Are the cute volcanoes really a selling point on this one? Does it really make it more fun? Couldn't the tiles just be shapes and the buildings just pieces?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Taluva reminds a bit about games like Java, Carcassonne and Torres but with much cleaner and more elegant mechanisms. You play on an island that is changed by tiles being placed next to other tiles or on top of them so that any previous settlement are partially removed. On this island you must play out your three kinds of settlements: ordinary huts, temples which require settlements of three areas and towers which require heights of at least three areas. The tiles can be used not only to block or harm other players but also to open up new areas or even split own settlements and allow the building of more temples and towers. In other words, strategy and tactics in many different dimensions!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
In this game, the tiles are composed of three terrain hexes. There is always a volcano and two different types of either lakes, woods, scrubs, and ore. Tiles may be placed on top of other tiles, provided the volcano flow is different. Players must place huts, temples or towers onto the tiles. Huts can be started individually, then expanded onto like terrain. Temples can go on a settlement of at least 3 huts. Towers can go on level 3 terrain. Tiles placed on top of other tiles may wipe out huts, but may never wipe out an entire settlement. The winner is the person to get all of 2 types of buildings on the board, or if the game ends when all tiles are drawn, the person who has the most temples. 
******
It's a quick tactical game, but I think the first player has an advantage, and there is the opportunity to play kingmaker. It's cute, but not one of those games that will get played often.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]Taluva[/b] was a TasCon 2017 auction purchase.

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8/10):[/COLOR]

[i]I'm going to have to stop saying I don't like abstracts; if you paste a good theme on it and have really nice components I'm in. Taluva joins Barony as another abstract I really enjoy, the fact that both feel like area control games definitely helps. The production is as good as Barony, which is saying a lot, the tiles and wooden pieces are beautiful. The tiles are also extremely thick so the expanding board you create looks impressive by the end of the game. 

The rules are deceptively simple and easily taught, but Taluva is a deep game with room for clever play. It can be just as advantageous to destroy your own pieces as well as your opponents, so the destruction of pieces doesn't feel particularly nasty. I wouldn't describe Taluva as a take that game, instead it you are constantly jockeying for board position as you race to place all of your pieces. 

Taluva has become an instant hit in my house, garnering a lot of plays in a short amount of time. I should also note that this game flies by with two players, we can get in a couple of games in a little over a half hour. Taluva will definitely be an evergreen in our collection.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Fun game that appears to scale well from 2 to 4 players without too much king-making, which I found surprising given the nature of the game.  It seems that squashing one person's holdings tends to just give them a better opportunity down the road, so great care must be taken before plunking down a tile just for the sake of 'hurting' someone.  I like it a lot, not least because the components are excellent.  Thick, chunky tiles and very pretty to boot, coupled with unique-looking wooden bits make this a game that nearly everyone stops to take a closer look at when it's on the table.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
規則:
*2/3/4人使用24/36/48個板塊(變體使用全部板塊)，板塊洗勻蓋著放一旁
*每人選一個顏色拿所有建築物，年輕玩家起始，順時針行動，每次行動兩個步驟
1.放置火山板塊 抽一個放，擴張至少一邊相鄰現有的，有空缺是允許的，火山爆發則放上面，重疊時上下的火山格必須完全重合，且方向不能一樣(至少要在橫跨兩塊板塊)，不能懸空，可蓋在房子上(被蓋房子移出遊戲)，其他建築不能被覆蓋，覆蓋殖民地至少要留一棟建築物
2.放置建築 至少放置一個建築到火山以外任意空格，連接兩個殖民地是允許的，一個獨立的建築物群落稱作殖民地。
>房舍  沒限制，可放在僅有一層的圖板上，放房舍可選擇擴張，選擇自己的一個殖民地，並選擇一種地形，所有與殖民地相鄰的該地形格都可以放置房舍，有幾層就放幾間房舍
>高塔  必須放在3層以上的圖板上，且要與舊有的殖民地相連，一個殖民地一座
>神廟  必須相鄰於3格(非3間房舍)以上的殖民地，一個殖民地一座
*放置神廟或高塔後導致殖民地相連而有兩座是允許的
*遊戲結束  
1.當有玩家蓋完三種建築物的兩種，該玩家立刻獲勝。
2.當玩家手上有建築物，卻無法依規定擺放任何建築物到圖板上，該玩家立刻淘汰出局
3.當所有火山板塊都被抽光遊戲結束，依序比神廟>高塔>房子，放最多的獲勝
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
A nice tile laying game that I find to be aesthetically very appealing. It's almost as if the goal of the game is to make it as beautiful looking as possible and if possible also earning some points. Very nice. It's actually more tactical then it might look at first. If you just play it the way you'd play carcassonne for example it's just a simple "race" game to see who's the first to dispatch his two types of buildings, but you can actually interfere quite heavily in an opponents game, since the towers and temples require specific conditions to be placed, you can actually, especially in the case of the temples mess in others' affairs. Can be quite interesting.

RATING: 8
Simple to learn, lots of interaction. A great game for anyone and works well with any number of players (2-4). You need to get rid of two types of your pieces by playing them on the board, which is being built by you and the others. But as two of the types of pieces have specific placement rules and others can use tiles and pieces to block you, there is a constant battle for location. It is a really nice looking game as well. They chose nice colors and the design was really though out so that it looks a certain way.

RATING: 9
Taluva is a three-dimensional tile-laying game in which players are trying to run out of two types of wooden tokens.  The three types of tokens all have different placement rules--huts are kind of generic, temples reward spreading out, and towers reward building up.  The triple-hex tiles can either be placed at the edge of the map, or on top of other tiles, potentially destroying existing tokens (which go out of the game).  It takes clever play to maximize your own placements while denying the expansion of others.  This is probably my favorite filler game.  I love the spatial thinking, and being forced to react to the play of others, both offensively and defensively.  Plus, the game is absolutely beautiful.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 9
For how simple the game is overall, it was actually a lot more challenging than I expected. Very aesthetically pleasing. The original version is just as excellent as the deluxe version. The only thing I've noticed in the deluxe version is larger pieces for everything. It looks nice, but it's too much of a table hog.

We did mess up a critical rule though that you cannot cover up a settlement completely. This wasn't too much of an issue, but it always felt odd to me that you could just kill off someone else's starting settlement. With the corrected rule in place, the game is much, much better.

Update: With repeat plays, I have to say this game has really grown on me. I liked it a lot on first blush, but it's just so quick playing and pretty. It's like a less involved Carcassonne with a upward spatial aspect which I really enjoy. A keeper and one I'm glad that I finally got to play. I almost passed this one by.

RATING: 8
Another MACM game I enjoy. Extremely lush production - just beautiful to watch the game unfold. Played aggressively, the 2P game has a lot of interaction and tactical challenges. Similar to Attika in weight and abstraction, though plays a bit quicker. I'm interested in picking up a 2nd set for Super-Taluva.

Update: There's a lot more tactical depth to this game than to Attika. The 2P game is largely a battle of tempo, with each player trying to set up build combos while blocking the other. There's definitely a learning curve in figuring out the best placements and responses. Learning how to use extra layers to make safe valleys is key, as is taking advantage of the fairly rare tiles with identical landscapes. This is an excellent game between two experienced players. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I just can't believe how a boardgame that's [i]so[/i] very simple can be so [i]sophisticated[/i] and [i]elegant[/i]. It's meaty yet quick and one of the most [i]beautiful[/i] games I've ever played. It's stunning to watch as the island, that begins from scratch, grows all the time ... and in [u]three dimensions[/u]. This is one of the most strategical and [i]nastiest[/i] [b]tile placement[/b] games I've experienced and the best part is that you can't play it without being really, really evil - [i]even if it's unintentional[/i]. There isn't anything that compares to this.

RATING: 9
The progressive volcanic birth of an island has to be one of the most satisfying board game mechanics ever devised.  Flow over your own huts to build another temple!  Provide a tempting target so that an opponent sets up a place to build a third level for your tower!  Place exactly the number of huts you have available by flowing into three different hexes!  And all along collectively give birth to a new world isolated in a sea of potential.  

So simple, so beautiful.  Another human invention I feel was ultimately inevitable.

RATING: 8
A suprisingly simple game that plays quickly with a heck of a lot of brain burning.  It's an interesting game of maniuplation and anticipation.  The game does look good, perhaps a bit convoluted and to its detriment, as the land pieces look so 'busy' that it can be hard to get a good overall picture of the board.

So what happens?  In this game, players are given a bunch of huts, a few temples, and then a couple of towers.  The goal is to try and use all of two kinds on the board before your opponent does.  If you can't do that, then at least have the most of the best kind on the board.. but be careful!  You have to be able to build at all times, and if you can't, you lose!

So, players take a tile and lay it down, anywhere on the board, provided that it touches with an already existing piece.  Eventually, the island will grow - upwards - via volcanoes.  Players will be able to play new tiles on top of existing onces.  This could possible displace some pieces while creating opportunity for others.

I recieved this in a trade and I quite like it.  It's simple as heck.  It would have been good if the game could support more than 4 players, but as is it's a decent title.  For now, I would be happy to play this again.

RATING: 7
Players take it in turns to place tiles that make up the Island, through a series of volcanic eruptions. They then place their meeples, which come in three different flavours. Each flavour has a different set of rules for how it is placed. And the simple but elegant objective is to be the first player to get rid of all of two different types of meeples.
This games strengths are its simple rules, quick play time, and the visual effect of the gradually increasing height of the island.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I like the look of this game. Each tile is in the shape of three hexes; one of the hexes is a volcano, and the others are various terrain types. Each turn players place a new tile and then make or expand settlements.

To play a tile, you can either expand into the ocean (adjacent to the board on at least one edge) or an existing volcano can erupt, allowing you to overlay the tile on existing tiles. Tiles can get as high as three "stories," although the placement rules make that challenging to do.

Placing a tile over existing tiles (erupting) is a little complicated: you overlay a tile so that the volcano hex on the tile being placed sits over the volcano hex on the tile underneath. Tiles may not "perfectly" overlay each other; that is, a tile must sit on top of at least two other tiles. You may not cover temples or towers, but you can cover huts: these huts are removed FROM THE GAME. You may not do this if it would cause an entire settlement to disappear.

To make a settlement you place a hut on an empty, non-volcano, first-story hex.

To expand settlements you can play a temple, tower, or hut adjacent to an existing settlement of yours. Towers may only be placed on a third story hex. Temples may only be added to settlements of at least three hexes in size. When huts are placed, you put one hut for each story. To expand with a hut, choose a terrain type that is adjacent to the settlement, and place huts in each adjacent hex of that terrain type. This is a fast way to get rid of your huts. You may only place a temple or tower next to a settlement that doesn't already have one, although it's okay if this action causes two settlements to merge, even if that gives it more than one temple or tower.

And you want to get rid of huts, but not as much as you want to get rid of temples and towers, because you score for having the fewest settlement pieces in your supply at the game end. The player with the fewest towers wins, or if that's a tie, then the fewest temples, etc. You can also instantly win the game if you have only one KIND of settlement piece left (e.g., all huts).

The distinction of "fewest in your pool" is important. You're not trying to get more pieces on the board, so much as you're trying to get rid of pieces. So placing your tiles on top of other people's huts doesn't hurt their score any (the huts are removed from the game), although it may impact their ability to add more pieces to the board.

I enjoyed the game and would be happy to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Definitely scores points for gorgeous-looking components. The tiles are thick and colourful and the wooden bits seem to be original and not recycled from another game. Gameplay however, disappoints. Players draw a tile to place each turn. Tiles have a variety of different landscaps that affect how settlements expand. Players then either place a new settlement or expand on an existing one. The object is to get get rid all the pieces of one type. Tile level, terrain and size of settlements affect how pieces can be placed.

What irked me was the volcano terrain. Each tile has a volcano terrain that can be used to destroy any settlement (with more than 1 village in it) it's placed over. This means that players can constantly do 'take that' moves if they want to. Someone who carefully places his settlements at the edges and flies under the radar will do well. If two players get in a Volcano war, expect them to hand the game to the other players. Kingmaking anyone?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's an abstract game, apparently with kingmaker problems if played with more than 2. Players have 3 types of tokens: a bunch of houses, 3 temples and 2 forts. Tiles are 3 hexes attached in a triangular setup, with different types of terrain. Each turn you must place a tile, then place a token (lose if you cannot). Tiles may be placed on top of each other, but never exactly on 1 tile. Houses are placed in all like terrain hexes (you pick type) adjacent to your village (=group of houses). Temples must be played on level 2, but only 1/village. Forts must be played on level 3 (only 1/village?). When laying tiles on other tiles, can play over houses (removed from game) but not temples of forts. First to play all pieces of 2 types wins!

The pieces are beautiful! The game is actually pretty good, although I don't care much for abstracts. If you do like those this is likely like up your alley!

6+/10

RATING: 8.2
BestWith:3>4>2. Very good with 2 (we use 2 sets of pieces each) [Abstract]

Björn's Description: "A fast three dimensional tile placement game. With really cute huts and volcanos, that can destroy the huts."

Use 12 tiles per player (times sets being played if playing multiple sets). We play Variants 1 and 2.

Variant 1: Two player, with each of us controlling 2 colors which are considered the same.

Variant 2: You cannot connect settlements if it will cause multiple towers or multiple temples in a settlement as a result.

Variant 3: Double Taluva: 3 towers, 4 temples, 30 huts, and 18 tiles per player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Thematic tile-placement abstract strategy game. On each turn players have to draw a tile with three different terrain types on them (one of which will be a volcano) and place them alongside or on top of other already placed tiles. They then place a building of three different types, each with their own placement rules (huts can only be placed on a level one tile, towers on a level three tile, temples next to a settlement consisting of three or more occupied land spaces etc) with the aim of exhausting their supply of two types of these buildings thereby winning the game. Both aspects of the gameplay require quite a lot of thought and decisions have to be made on whether it's best to place tiles to benefit yourself or to hinder your opponents. The theme fits well into the game and strong strategic play is generally rewarded making this overall a good abstract placement game.

RATING: 8
tl;dr: classic style euro

This is kind of the quintessential clean, elegant, classic euro: few components, few rules, good deep strategy and tactics.
This game has one type of tiles (with different terrain types) and some wooden bits for each player. Everything has some fairly easy to grasp placing rules and there are fairly easy end game conditions and scoring. It all comes together in a fairly short but very replayable game with good strategy and tactics.
This game doesn't work great with 3 if 2 players go head to head, as the third player will come out on top. But in games with minimal interaction it works great with all player counts. I think it's also excellent with 2.

Rating: 8/10
BGG scale: 8/10

RATING: 9
I had a two-player learning game last night.  First impression of this game is how utterly BEAUTIFUL it is.  Rules/mechanics are quite easy to pick up, and the game plays fast.  Who knows how it will hold up in replay value, but for now I eagerly await my next game.

Update: Played one 3 player and one 4 player session yesterday, and we had so much fun I just had to bump this up to a 9.  3 player was markedly more exciting than 2 player (but 2 player still more strategic), and 4 player was utter CHAOS but made you think about every move you were doing!  What a great game.

Update: Played another 4 player game.  This one was enjoyable as well.  I am bumping this down slightly because this game was rather bumpy.  Specifically, one player kind of made his own little peninsula late in the game, and none of us were able to counter it without scheming amongst one another.  Kind of a weird "kingmaker" feeling resulted.  Oh well.  Still a ton of fun to play and will never decline a game.

RATING: 5.5
First Impression:

A great light tile-laying game.

There is some good strategy here and the game flows well and very quick. The final result with the multiple levels is also very attractive.

I didn't like the possible player elimination near the end of the game. It's ok to slow down a runaway leader (which however is not at all an issue in this game) but still it feels cheap when you're suddenly out because of slight miscalculation or an aggressive enemy block. It's less of an issue however in such a quick game.

All in all I did like this game.
