About [thing=24508][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=24508][/thing] is a tile-laying game in which players put Huts, Temples or Towers, each with their own restrictions and requirements (Temples demand a settlement of at least 3 Huts; a Tower must be placed in a third-level tile).

The game has very simple rules, but the placement of tiles, the actions of expansion of the settlement and putting other buildings, allow for a good amount of depth and some strategy, mixed with a lot of tatical play, since you don't know which tile you will be using next.

There is direct interaction during play, since the addition of second and third level tiles can, and usually do, cover and take out Huts of other players, normally preventing them from being able to build a Temple. However, is unlike that the damage done is too harmful: is more often simply a bump in the road, a delay. as settlements can't be fully destroyed. Yet, as [thing=24508][/thing] is pretty tight, is possible that a small delay of one turn can mean losing the game. It is the nature of the thing: one mustn't losing opportunities.

The production value of this new printing is great: the tiles are thick, sturdy and colorful; the pieces are of wood and nice to the touch; the box is huge and keeps everything in place - but is a hassle to put everything back once the game is done.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
As per the description: each turn, players decide to either have a new volcano erupt along the shore, increasing the size of the island, or to have an existing volcano erupt again, increasing the height of the land around it (and possibly destroying parts of existing settlements). They do this by placing a new tile, consisting of one volcano and two other types of landscape. A tile must always touch at least one other tile, when placed at sea level, or be placed on top of at least two other tiles (without any gaps under the land being created), with the volcano being placed on top of an existing volcano.

Next, the player will place one or more wooden buildings; huts, temples or towers. Settlements must always start at the lowest level, by placing a single hut. From there on, existing settlements may expand by placing huts on all hexes of a single type of terrain around the settlement, with temples once the settlement takes up at least three hexes, or with towers, placed at level three or above.

The game ends when all tiles have been placed. At that point, the player who's placed most temples wins. Ties are broken by towers, then huts. Ultimate victory - and an immediate end to the game - waits for the player who manages to place all their buildings of two types. Immediate defeat is also possible, when no buildings can legally be played during a player's turn.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
The main one is: where to put the tile drawn. This has several considerations: board state (the formation and what the others and you have on the board and where), what the player wants to do (expand to add more Huts, put a Temple or a Tower, block someone else, split a settlement, etc) and, finally, what she doesn't want to do (this is important, as laying a tile can be more useful to the others than to yourself, as they can use it to place tiles in the second or third level).

[thing=24508][/thing] usually requires forethought from the players: you are laying the foundations of your next plays right now. This isn't a complex and intricate demand, but sure carries weight and is key to doing well.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Good production value;
- Language independent;
- Easy to teach and to play, but with a good amount of depth in the gameplay;
- Tight game, with the decision often coming in the final round (and this is a game that can end early, not with a predefined number of rounds);
- Constant interaction and fight for space - the destruction of Huts isn't, normally, crippling or too harmful;
- Short playing time with a good pacing and little downtime;
- Is nice too see the evolution of the board, growing and rising, dotted with buildings.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Mostly abstract gameplay.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like playing a little more complex version of [thing=822][/thing]: [thing=24508][/thing] has more types of things to add (three or four types of buildings), more ways to place tiles (in three levels), and is more agressive, but the heart of it is the same: lay tiles and put stuff on them. There is no cost to this, no economy - at the most a small amout of resource management: with meeples and Huts (though finishing the Huts can be good, is also very possible to not be able to build in one of the next turns and be eliminated). It is hard for someone that enjoys [thing=822][/thing] to be displeased by [thing=24508][/thing].

Overall, [thing=24508][/thing] offers a fine mix of simple rules, good depth, a nice amount of interaction, and a lively pacing, with short downtime, all put in a pretty to look at and handle package.

RATING: 6
12/2010:4
I am a fan of Merkle's design and Attika is an old favorite of mine. However, Taluva really fell flat for me. The game play was plain and as my partner commented "needlessly complicated". While the game play is not complex, something does not feel right.It feels like you have many options for tile play but when it comes down to placement of buildings, it is really tough to prevent your opponent from placing or building their pieces since they get a fresh tile placement every turn. This means that you can agonize about how to destroy your opponent's moves or block their placement, but it is really hard to do that without sacrificing your turn. This means that the game has a very solitaire feel to it and it is just a race to the end to see who finishes first. Not really very enticing for me. A pass for me.

7/2016:6
There is an elegance to the game, but also a certain amount of cuthroatness to it. I have reacquired, replayed and then sold off the game again, twice around. After my initial sale, I thought perhaps I made a mistake. The game should appeal to me: the simple Knizia-esque ruleset, the strong spatial tile-placing elements and a highly acclaimed designer whose previous designs I thoroughly enjoyed.  While I didn't dislike the game, I wasn't exactly moved by it. Player who enjoy the slow buildup felt frustrated often by more aggressive players who constantly sought to take advantage of tile placement to destroy villages. While this can indeed be attributed to clever or superior play, it clearly destroyed the fun for some players who couldn't maintain presence on the board. This feature will be a turn off for some and a turn on for others. Getting the correct group to play this game will be crucial for all around enjoyment.

RATING: 8
It sort of feels like Carcassone plus volcanoes equals Taluva.  Not that that's a bad thing; I think that Taluva represents a new and interesting direction for groups who like tile laying games but for whom Carcassone has worn out its welcome.

I really like how the game-end condition is in the hands of the players and that their is a clearly defined long-term strategic element (which types of buildings will you try to build) and a host of short-term tactical elements (where to place your tiles, to try to build your buildings or prevent your opponents from doing the same, etc.) that have to be taken into consideration if you want to be able to accomplish your strategic goals.

Last but not least, the theme in Taluva is great and it is incorporated into the mechanics (volcanoes anyone?) seamlessly.

RATING: 8
A pleasant surprise.  I eschew most tile-laying titles as too light, but this game has just enough to satisfy me.  While [gameid=822] fails to hold my attention or offer enough choices, Taluva offers strategic and tactical flexibility.  The variable endpoint makes the game much more tense, in my estimation. You can hamper other players, but you have to be careful, because it may ultimately help them by giving them more space to expand.  It's clever that way.  This game adapts well to the playstyle of the group, which is great for both gaming with your most cutthroat get-together, or as a lighter endeavor with some more casual-minded friends.

RATING: 8
The theme of building an island from an erupting volcano works well for a game that's abstract. There's plenty of strategy in Taluva but those who enjoy building for fun will like playing too. The rules are simple but there's quite a few of them to remember, making the first play a learning game. Fortunately the game plays quickly. The weak player in a multiplayer game can hand the win to an opponent but games are so short, it's a minor annoyance.

RATING: 9
Quick to explain and play... deep abstractish feel... gorgeous components... scales well and a joy to play!  

I particularly like that while drawing certain tiles can be more or less helpful with a tribe expansion, most of the time you can make the play you want regardless of the terrain shown on the tile.  Finding positions where your tribes are safe and can expand the way you want them too is very engaging and covering you opponents (and occasionally yourself) in lava can get pleasantly vicious.

I'm keeping track of my quest to play 100 games in this geeklist: [url]
http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/142757/item/2164793#item2164793
[/url]

RATING: 9.5
Underrated area control game.  If you can get past the name and ugly cover art, this is a great, simple, fast playing game.  The key to winning is balancing placing tiles to build settlements while at the same time blocking opponents from building temples and towers.  There's a lot more strategy in how you actually place a tile too because the location and orientation can reduce your vulnerability to having an opponent destroy some of your huts.  Can get nasty when your volcano messes up your opponent's settlement.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After 10+ Plays: The first game in a long time that I went out and bought right after playing it.  The pieces are beautiful, and the mechanics are really interesting.  Just as in this designer's other game [gameid=8051] the direct player interaction and non-VP-oriented winning conditions are a nice change of pace from many of the other Euro's.  I really like how there is very little luck, even though it is a tile-laying game, because in essence all the tiles are the same except in regards to one of your action choices.  A win will usually come from good strategic planning, and not from drawing the right tile at the right time.
